      <h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Economy">Economy</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Turkmenistan&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: Economy">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2> 
      <div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">
       Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Economy_of_Turkmenistan" title="Economy of Turkmenistan">Economy of Turkmenistan</a> and <a href="/wiki/Agriculture_in_Turkmenistan" title="Agriculture in Turkmenistan">Agriculture in Turkmenistan</a>
      </div> 
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
        <a href="/wiki/File:Turkmenistan_Export_Treemap.png" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/ff/Turkmenistan_Export_Treemap.png/220px-Turkmenistan_Export_Treemap.png" decoding="async" width="220" height="172" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/ff/Turkmenistan_Export_Treemap.png/330px-Turkmenistan_Export_Treemap.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/ff/Turkmenistan_Export_Treemap.png/440px-Turkmenistan_Export_Treemap.png 2x" data-file-width="961" data-file-height="750"></a> 
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a href="/wiki/File:Turkmenistan_Export_Treemap.png" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a>
         </div>Graphical depiction of Turkmenistan's product exports in 28 color-coded categories
        </div>
       </div>
      </div> 
      <p>The country possesses the world's fourth largest reserves of <a href="/wiki/Natural_gas" title="Natural gas">natural gas</a> and substantial oil resources.<sup id="cite_ref-98" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-98">[98]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>Turkmenistan has taken a cautious approach to economic reform, hoping to use gas and cotton sales to sustain its economy. In 2014, the unemployment rate was estimated to be 11%.<sup id="cite_ref-World_Factbook_5-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-World_Factbook-5">[5]</a></sup> </p> 
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
        <a href="/wiki/File:Bai_Bazaar,_Dashoguz.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/49/Bai_Bazaar%2C_Dashoguz.jpg/220px-Bai_Bazaar%2C_Dashoguz.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="165" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/49/Bai_Bazaar%2C_Dashoguz.jpg/330px-Bai_Bazaar%2C_Dashoguz.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/49/Bai_Bazaar%2C_Dashoguz.jpg/440px-Bai_Bazaar%2C_Dashoguz.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1024" data-file-height="768"></a> 
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a href="/wiki/File:Bai_Bazaar,_Dashoguz.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a>
         </div><a href="/wiki/Bazaar" title="Bazaar">Bazaar</a> in <a href="/wiki/Da%C5%9Foguz" title="Daşoguz">Daşoguz</a>
        </div>
       </div>
      </div> 
      <p>Between 1998 and 2002, Turkmenistan suffered from the continued lack of adequate <a href="/wiki/Export" title="Export">export</a> routes for natural gas and from obligations on extensive short-term external <a href="/wiki/Debt" title="Debt">debt</a>. At the same time, however, the value of total exports rose sharply due to increases in international oil and gas prices. The subsequent collapse of both hydrocarbon and cotton prices in 2014 cut revenues from export sales severely, causing Turkmenistan to run trade deficits from 2015 through 2017.<sup id="cite_ref-stat2020_99-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-stat2020-99">[99]</a></sup> Economic prospects in the near future are discouraging because of widespread internal <a href="/wiki/Poverty" title="Poverty">poverty</a> and the burden of foreign debt,<sup id="cite_ref-100" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-100">[100]</a></sup> coupled with continued low hydrocarbon prices and reduced Chinese purchases of natural gas.<sup id="cite_ref-fpri_101-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-fpri-101">[101]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-eurnetgas_102-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-eurnetgas-102">[102]</a></sup> One reflection of economic stress is the black-market exchange rate for the Turkmen manat, which though officially set at 3.5 manat to the U.S. dollar, reportedly was trading in January 2021 at 32 manat to the dollar.<sup id="cite_ref-manat_103-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-manat-103">[103]</a></sup> </p> 
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
        <a href="/wiki/File:10000_manat._T%C3%BCrkmenistan,_1998_b.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/15/10000_manat._T%C3%BCrkmenistan%2C_1998_b.jpg/220px-10000_manat._T%C3%BCrkmenistan%2C_1998_b.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="110" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/15/10000_manat._T%C3%BCrkmenistan%2C_1998_b.jpg/330px-10000_manat._T%C3%BCrkmenistan%2C_1998_b.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/15/10000_manat._T%C3%BCrkmenistan%2C_1998_b.jpg/440px-10000_manat._T%C3%BCrkmenistan%2C_1998_b.jpg 2x" data-file-width="7644" data-file-height="3822"></a> 
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a href="/wiki/File:10000_manat._T%C3%BCrkmenistan,_1998_b.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a>
         </div><a href="/wiki/Saparmurat_Hajji_Mosque" title="Saparmurat Hajji Mosque">Saparmurat Hajji Mosque</a> featured on the obsolete 10,000 manat banknote.
        </div>
       </div>
      </div> 
      <p>President Niyazov spent much of the country's revenue on extensively renovating cities, Ashgabat in particular. Corruption watchdogs voiced particular concern over the management of Turkmenistan's currency reserves, most of which are held in off-budget funds such as the Foreign Exchange Reserve Fund in the <a href="/wiki/Deutsche_Bank" title="Deutsche Bank">Deutsche Bank</a> in <a href="/wiki/Frankfurt" title="Frankfurt">Frankfurt</a>, according to a report released in April 2006 by London-based non-governmental organization <a href="/wiki/Global_Witness" title="Global Witness">Global Witness</a>. </p>
      <p>According to a decree of the Peoples' Council of 14 August 2003,<sup id="cite_ref-104" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-104">[104]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Electricity" title="Electricity">electricity</a>, <a href="/wiki/Natural_gas" title="Natural gas">natural gas</a>, <a href="/wiki/Water" title="Water">water</a> and <a href="/wiki/Salt" title="Salt">salt</a> were to have been subsidized for citizens until 2030. Under implementing regulations, every citizen was entitled to 35 kilowatt hours of electricity and 50 cubic meters of natural gas each month. The state also provided 250 liters (66 gallons) of water per day.<sup id="cite_ref-105" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-105">[105]</a></sup> As of 1 January 2019, however, all such subsidies were abolished, and payment for utilities was implemented.<sup id="cite_ref-subsidy1_106-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-subsidy1-106">[106]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-subsidy2_107-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-subsidy2-107">[107]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-subsidy3_108-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-subsidy3-108">[108]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-fines_109-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-fines-109">[109]</a></sup> </p> 
      <h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Natural_gas_and_export_routes">Natural gas and export routes</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Turkmenistan&amp;action=edit&amp;section=14" title="Edit section: Natural gas and export routes">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3> 
      <div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">
       Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Economy_of_Turkmenistan#Natural_gas" title="Economy of Turkmenistan">Economy of Turkmenistan §&nbsp;Natural gas</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Pipelines_in_Turkmenistan" title="Pipelines in Turkmenistan">Pipelines in Turkmenistan</a>
      </div> 
      <div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">
       See also: <a href="/wiki/List_of_countries_by_natural_gas_proven_reserves" title="List of countries by natural gas proven reserves">List of countries by natural gas proven reserves</a> and <a href="/wiki/List_of_countries_by_natural_gas_exports" title="List of countries by natural gas exports">List of countries by natural gas exports</a>
      </div> 
      <p>As of May 2011<sup class="plainlinks noexcerpt noprint asof-tag update" style="display:none;"><a class="external text" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Turkmenistan&amp;action=edit">[update]</a></sup>, the <a href="/wiki/Galkynysh_Gas_Field" title="Galkynysh Gas Field">Galkynysh Gas Field</a> was estimated to possess the second-largest volume of gas in the world, after the <a href="/wiki/South_Pars/North_Dome_Gas-Condensate_field" title="South Pars/North Dome Gas-Condensate field">South Pars</a> field in the Persian Gulf. Reserves at the <a href="/wiki/Galkynysh_Gas_Field" title="Galkynysh Gas Field">Galkynysh Gas Field</a> are estimated at around 21.2 trillion cubic metres.<sup id="cite_ref-110" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-110">[110]</a></sup> The Turkmenistan Natural Gas Company (<a href="/wiki/T%C3%BCrkmengaz" title="Türkmengaz">Türkmengaz</a>) controls gas extraction in the country. Gas production is the most dynamic and promising sector of the national economy.<sup id="cite_ref-Turkmenistan_Gas_Production_111-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Turkmenistan_Gas_Production-111">[111]</a></sup> In 2009 the government of Turkmenistan began a policy of diversifying export routes for its raw materials.<sup id="cite_ref-europarussia.com_112-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-europarussia.com-112">[112]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>Prior to 1958 gas production was limited to <a href="/wiki/Natural_gas#Natural_gas" title="Natural gas">associated gas</a> from oil wells in western Turkmenistan. In 1958, the first gas wells were drilled at Serhetabat (then Kushky) and at Derweze.<sup id="cite_ref-geotext_89-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-geotext-89">[89]</a></sup> Oil and gas fields were discovered in the Central Karakum Desert between 1959 and 1965. In addition to Derweze, these include Takyr, Shyh, Chaljulba, Topjulba, Chemmerli, Atabay, Sakarchage, Atasary, Mydar, Goyun, and Zakli. These fields are located in Jurassic and Cretaceous sediments.<sup id="cite_ref-geotext_89-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-geotext-89">[89]</a></sup> The Turkmen gas industry got underway with the opening of the Ojak gas field in 1966. To put this in perspective, associated gas production in Turkmenistan was only 1.157 billion cubic meters in 1965, but by 1970 natural gas production reached 13 billion cubic meters, and by 1989, 90 billion cubic meters. The USSR exported much of this gas to western Europe. Following independence, natural gas extraction fell as Turkmenistan sought export markets but was limited to existing delivery infrastructure under Russian control: Turkmenistan-Russia in two lines (3087&nbsp;km, originating at Ojak, and another of 2259&nbsp;km, also originating at Ojak); the Gumdag line (2530&nbsp;km); and the Shatlyk line (2644&nbsp;km) to Russia, Ukraine, and the Caucasus.<sup id="cite_ref-geotext_89-7" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-geotext-89">[89]</a></sup> On 1 January 2016, Russia halted natural gas purchases from Turkmenistan after reducing them step by step for the previous years.<sup id="cite_ref-stop_113-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-stop-113">[113]</a></sup> Russia's <a href="/wiki/Gazprom" title="Gazprom">Gazprom</a> announced resumption of purchases in April 2019, but reported volumes remained low compared to previous delivery levels.<sup id="cite_ref-rferl_114-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-rferl-114">[114]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>In 1997, the <a href="/wiki/Korpeje%E2%80%93Kordkuy_pipeline" title="Korpeje–Kordkuy pipeline">Korpeje-Gurtguy</a> natural gas pipeline was built to <a href="/wiki/Iran" title="Iran">Iran</a>. It is 140 kilometers in length and was the first gas pipeline to a foreign customer constructed after independence.<sup id="cite_ref-geotext_89-8" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-geotext-89">[89]</a></sup> Turkmenistan's exports of natural gas to Iran, estimated at about 12 bcma, ended on January 1, 2017, when <a href="/wiki/Turkmengaz" class="mw-redirect" title="Turkmengaz">Turkmengaz</a> unilaterally cut off deliveries, citing payment arrears.<sup id="cite_ref-iran_115-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-iran-115">[115]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-iran2_116-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-iran2-116">[116]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>In December 2009 the first line, Line A, of the <a href="/wiki/Central_Asia%E2%80%93China_gas_pipeline" title="Central Asia–China gas pipeline">Trans-Asia</a> pipeline to China opened, creating a second major market for Turkmen natural gas. By 2015 Turkmenistan was delivering up to 35 billion cubic meters per annum (bcma) to China.<sup id="cite_ref-cnpc_117-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-cnpc-117">[117]</a></sup> China is the largest buyer of gas from Turkmenistan, via three pipelines linking the two countries through Uzbekistan and Kazakhstan. In 2019, China bought over 30bcm of gas from Turkmenistan,<sup id="cite_ref-118" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-118">[118]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-119" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-119">[119]</a></sup> making China Turkmenistan's main external source of revenue.<sup id="cite_ref-120" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-120">[120]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>The <a href="/wiki/East%E2%80%93West_pipeline" title="East–West pipeline">East–West pipeline</a> was completed in December 2015, with the intent of delivering up to 30 bcm of natural gas to the Caspian shore for eventual export through a yet-to-be-built <a href="/wiki/Trans-Caspian_Gas_Pipeline" title="Trans-Caspian Gas Pipeline">Trans-Caspian natural gas pipeline</a> connecting the Belek-1 compressor station in Turkmenistan to Azerbaijan. </p>
      <p>The Turkmenistan government continues to pursue construction of the <a href="/wiki/Turkmenistan%E2%80%93Afghanistan%E2%80%93Pakistan%E2%80%93India_Pipeline" title="Turkmenistan–Afghanistan–Pakistan–India Pipeline">Turkmenistan–Afghanistan–Pakistan–India Pipeline</a>, or TAPI.<sup id="cite_ref-:0_121-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:0-121">[121]</a></sup> The anticipated cost of the TAPI pipeline is currently estimated at $25 billion. Turkmenistan's section of the pipeline was started in 2015 and was completed in 2019, though the Afghanistan and Pakistan sections remain under construction. </p> 
      <h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Oil">Oil</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Turkmenistan&amp;action=edit&amp;section=15" title="Edit section: Oil">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3> 
      <div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">
       Main article: <a href="/wiki/Economy_of_Turkmenistan#Oil" title="Economy of Turkmenistan">Economy of Turkmenistan §&nbsp;Oil</a>
      </div> 
      <div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">
       See also: <a href="/wiki/Pipelines_in_Turkmenistan" title="Pipelines in Turkmenistan">Pipelines in Turkmenistan</a>
      </div> 
      <p>Oil was known to exist in western Turkmenistan as early as the 18th century. General <a href="/wiki/Aleksey_Kuropatkin" title="Aleksey Kuropatkin">Aleksey Kuropatkin</a> reported in 1879 that the <a href="/wiki/Cheleken_Peninsula" title="Cheleken Peninsula">Cheleken Peninsula</a> had as many as three thousand oil sources.<sup id="cite_ref-kuropatkin_122-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-kuropatkin-122">[122]</a></sup> Turkmen settlers in the 19th century extracted oil near the surface and shipped it to <a href="/wiki/Astrakhan" title="Astrakhan">Astrakhan</a> by ship and to Iran by camel caravan. Commercial oil drilling began in the 1890s. The oil extraction industry grew with the exploitation of the fields in Cheleken in 1909 (by <a href="/wiki/Branobel" title="Branobel">Branobel</a>) and in Balkanabat in the 1930s. Production leaped ahead with the discovery of the Gumdag field in 1948 and the Goturdepe field in 1959. By 1940 production had reached two million tons per year, by 1960 over four million tons, and by 1970 over 14 million tons. Oil production in 2019 was 9.8 million tons.<sup id="cite_ref-geotext_89-9" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-geotext-89">[89]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-stat2020_99-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-stat2020-99">[99]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>Oil wells are mainly found in the western lowlands. This area also produces associated natural gas. The main oilfields are Cheleken, Gonurdepe, Nebitdag, Gumdag, Barsagelmez, Guyujyk, Gyzylgum, Ordekli, Gogerendag, Gamyshlyja, Ekerem, Chekishler, Keymir, Ekizek, and Bugdayly. Oil is also produced from offshore wells in the Caspian Sea.<sup id="cite_ref-geotext_89-10" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-geotext-89">[89]</a></sup> Most oil is extracted by the Turkmenistan State Company (Concern) <a href="/wiki/T%C3%BCrkmennebit" title="Türkmennebit">Türkmennebit</a> from fields at Goturdepe, <a href="/wiki/Balkanabat" title="Balkanabat">Balkanabat</a>, and on the <a href="/wiki/Hazar,_Turkmenistan" title="Hazar, Turkmenistan">Cheleken</a> Peninsula near the Caspian Sea, which have a combined estimated reserve of 700 million tons. Much of the oil produced in Turkmenistan is refined in the <a href="/wiki/Turkmenbashy" class="mw-redirect" title="Turkmenbashy">Turkmenbashy</a> and <a href="/wiki/Se%C3%BDdi" title="Seýdi">Seydi</a> refineries. Some oil is exported by tanker vessel across the Caspian Sea en route to Europe via <a href="/wiki/Baku" title="Baku">Baku</a> and <a href="/wiki/Makhachkala" title="Makhachkala">Makhachkala</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-123" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-123">[123]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-vitol_124-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-vitol-124">[124]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-socar_125-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-socar-125">[125]</a></sup> Foreign firms involved in offshore oil extraction include <a href="/wiki/Eni" title="Eni">Eni S.p.A.</a> of Italy, <a href="/wiki/Dragon_Oil" title="Dragon Oil">Dragon Oil</a> of the United Arab Emirates, and <a href="/wiki/Petronas" title="Petronas">Petronas</a> of Malaysia. </p>
      <p>On 21 January 2021, the governments of <a href="/wiki/Azerbaijan" title="Azerbaijan">Azerbaijan</a> and Turkmenistan signed a memorandum of understanding to develop jointly an oil field in the <a href="/wiki/Caspian_Sea" title="Caspian Sea">Caspian Sea</a> that straddles the nations' border. Known previously as Kyapaz in <a href="/wiki/Azerbaijani_language" title="Azerbaijani language">Azeri</a> and Serdar in Turkmen, the oil field, now called Dostluk ("friendship" in both languages), potentially has reserves of up to 60 million tons of oil as well as associated natural gas.<sup id="cite_ref-ogj_126-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ogj-126">[126]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-enet_127-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-enet-127">[127]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-rfe_128-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-rfe-128">[128]</a></sup> </p> 
      <h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Energy">Energy</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Turkmenistan&amp;action=edit&amp;section=16" title="Edit section: Energy">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3> 
      <div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">
       See also: <a href="/wiki/Economy_of_Turkmenistan#Power_generation" title="Economy of Turkmenistan">Economy of Turkmenistan §&nbsp;Power generation</a>
      </div> 
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:302px;">
        <a href="/wiki/File:Gorskii_04414u.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/74/Gorskii_04414u.jpg/300px-Gorskii_04414u.jpg" decoding="async" width="300" height="271" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/74/Gorskii_04414u.jpg/450px-Gorskii_04414u.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/74/Gorskii_04414u.jpg/600px-Gorskii_04414u.jpg 2x" data-file-width="3329" data-file-height="3010"></a> 
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a href="/wiki/File:Gorskii_04414u.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a>
         </div>The generators of the Hindukush hydro power plant
        </div>
       </div>
      </div> 
      <p>Turkmenistan's first electrical power plant was built in 1909 and went into full operation in 1913. As of 2019 it was still in operation. The original triple-turbine Hindukush hydroelectric plant, built by the Austro-Hungarian company Hans on the Murghab River, was designed to produce 1.2 megawatts at 16.5 kilovolts.<sup id="cite_ref-hindukush_129-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-hindukush-129">[129]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-minenergo_130-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-minenergo-130">[130]</a></sup> Until 1957, however, most electrical power in Turkmenistan was produced locally by small Diesel generators and Diesel-electric locomotives.<sup id="cite_ref-minenergo_130-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-minenergo-130">[130]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>In 1957 Soviet authorities created a republic-level directorate for power generation, and in 1966 Turkmenistan entered the first phase of connecting its remote regions to the regional Central Asian electrical grid. By 1979 all rural areas of Turkmenistan were brought on line. Construction of the Mary thermal power plant began in 1969, and by 1987 the eighth and final generator block was completed, bringing the plant to its design capacity of 1.686 gigawatts. In 1998 Turkmenenergo commissioned its first gas-turbine power plant, using <a href="/wiki/GE" class="mw-redirect" title="GE">GE</a> turbines.<sup id="cite_ref-minenergo_130-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-minenergo-130">[130]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>As of 2010 Turkmenistan featured eight major power plants operating on natural gas, in <a href="/wiki/Mary,_Turkmenistan" title="Mary, Turkmenistan">Mary</a>, <a href="/wiki/Ashgabat" title="Ashgabat">Ashgabat</a>, <a href="/wiki/Balkanabat" title="Balkanabat">Balkanabat</a>, <a href="/wiki/B%C3%BCzme%C3%BDin,_Turkmenistan" title="Büzmeýin, Turkmenistan">Buzmeyin</a> (suburb of Ashgabat), <a href="/wiki/Da%C5%9Foguz" title="Daşoguz">Dashoguz</a>, <a href="/wiki/T%C3%BCrkmenba%C5%9Fy,_Turkmenistan" title="Türkmenbaşy, Turkmenistan">Turkmenbashy</a>, <a href="/wiki/T%C3%BCrkmenabat" title="Türkmenabat">Turkmenabat</a>, and Seydi.<sup id="cite_ref-geotext_89-11" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-geotext-89">[89]</a></sup> As of 2013, Turkmenistan had 10 electrical power plants equipped with 32 turbines, including 14 steam-driven, 15 gas powered, and 3 hydroelectric.<sup id="cite_ref-plan_131-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-plan-131">[131]</a></sup> Power output in 2011 was 18.27 billion kWh, of which 2.5 billion kWh was exported.<sup id="cite_ref-plan_131-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-plan-131">[131]</a></sup> Major power generating installations include the Hindukush Hydroelectric Station,<sup id="cite_ref-132" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-132">[132]</a></sup> which has a rated capacity of 350 megawatts, and the Mary Thermoelectric Power Station,<sup id="cite_ref-133" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-133">[133]</a></sup> which has a rated capacity of 1,370 megawatts. In 2018, electrical power production totaled more than 21 billion kilowatt-hours.<sup id="cite_ref-134" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-134">[134]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>Since 2013, additional power plants have been constructed in <a href="/wiki/Mary,_Turkmenistan" title="Mary, Turkmenistan">Mary</a> and <a href="/wiki/Ahal_Region" title="Ahal Region">Ahal province</a>, and another is under construction in <a href="/wiki/%C3%87%C3%A4rjew_District" title="Çärjew District">Çärjew District</a> of <a href="/wiki/Lebap_Region" title="Lebap Region">Lebap province</a>. The Mary-3 combined cycle power plant, built by <a href="/wiki/%C3%87al%C4%B1k_Holding" title="Çalık Holding">Çalık Holding</a> with <a href="/wiki/GE" class="mw-redirect" title="GE">GE</a> turbines, commissioned in 2018, produces 1.574 gigawatts of electrical power and is specifically intended to support expanded exports of electricity to Afghanistan and Pakistan. The Zerger power plant under construction by <a href="/wiki/Sumitomo" class="mw-redirect" title="Sumitomo">Sumitomo</a>, <a href="/wiki/Mitsubishi" title="Mitsubishi">Mitsubishi</a>, <a href="/wiki/Hitachi" title="Hitachi">Hitachi</a>, and <a href="/wiki/R%C3%B6nesans_Holding" title="Rönesans Holding">Rönesans Holding</a> in Çärjew District will have a design capacity of 432 megawatts. It is also primarily intended for export of electricity. The Ahal power plant, with capacity of 650 megawatts, was constructed to power the city of Ashgabat and in particular the Olympic Village.<sup id="cite_ref-orient1_135-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-orient1-135">[135]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-ahal1_136-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ahal1-136">[136]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-trend1_137-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-trend1-137">[137]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-zerger_138-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-zerger-138">[138]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>Turkmenistan is a net exporter of electrical power to Central Asian republics and southern neighbors. In 2019, total electrical energy generation in Turkmenistan reportedly totaled 22,521.6 million kilowatt-hours (22.52 terawatt-hours).<sup id="cite_ref-stat2020p_139-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-stat2020p-139">[139]</a></sup> </p> 
      <h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Agriculture">Agriculture</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Turkmenistan&amp;action=edit&amp;section=17" title="Edit section: Agriculture">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3> 
      <div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">
       Main article: <a href="/wiki/Agriculture_in_Turkmenistan" title="Agriculture in Turkmenistan">Agriculture in Turkmenistan</a>
      </div> 
      <p>Following independence in 1991, Soviet-era <a href="/wiki/Kolkhoz" title="Kolkhoz">collective-</a> and <a href="/wiki/Sovkhoz" title="Sovkhoz">state farms</a> were converted to "farmers associations" (<a href="/wiki/Turkmen_language" title="Turkmen language">Turkmen</a>: <span lang="tk">daýhan birleşigi</span>).<sup id="cite_ref-geotext_89-12" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-geotext-89">[89]</a></sup> Virtually all field crops are irrigated due to the aridity of the climate. The top crop in terms of area planted is wheat (761 thousand hectares in 2019), followed by cotton (551 thousand hectares in 2019).<sup id="cite_ref-stat2020_99-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-stat2020-99">[99]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>Turkmenistan is the world's tenth-largest cotton producer.<sup id="cite_ref-140" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-140">[140]</a></sup> Turkmenistan started producing cotton in the Murghab Valley following conquest of <a href="/wiki/Mary,_Turkmenistan" title="Mary, Turkmenistan">Merv</a> by the <a href="/wiki/Russian_Empire" title="Russian Empire">Russian Empire</a> in 1884.<sup id="cite_ref-141" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-141">[141]</a></sup> According to human rights organizations, public sector workers, such as teachers and doctors, are required by the government to pick cotton under the threat of losing their jobs if they refuse.<sup id="cite_ref-142" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-142">[142]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>During the 2020 season, Turkmenistan reportedly produced roughly 1.5 million tons of raw cotton. In 2012, around 7,000 tractors, 5,000 cotton cultivators, 2,200 sowing machines and other machinery, mainly procured from Belarus and the United States, were used. Prior to imposition of a ban on export of raw cotton in October 2018, Turkmenistan exported raw cotton to Russia, Iran, South Korea, United Kingdom, China, Indonesia, Turkey, Ukraine, Singapore and the <a href="/wiki/Baltic_states" title="Baltic states">Baltic states</a>. Beginning in 2019, the Turkmenistan government shifted focus to export of cotton yarn and finished textiles and garments.<sup id="cite_ref-143" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-143">[143]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-exportban_144-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-exportban-144">[144]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-orient_145-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-orient-145">[145]</a></sup> </p> 
      <h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Tourism">Tourism</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Turkmenistan&amp;action=edit&amp;section=18" title="Edit section: Tourism">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3> 
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
        <a href="/wiki/File:Darvasa_gas_crater_panorama.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8a/Darvasa_gas_crater_panorama.jpg/220px-Darvasa_gas_crater_panorama.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="93" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8a/Darvasa_gas_crater_panorama.jpg/330px-Darvasa_gas_crater_panorama.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8a/Darvasa_gas_crater_panorama.jpg/440px-Darvasa_gas_crater_panorama.jpg 2x" data-file-width="6042" data-file-height="2567"></a> 
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a href="/wiki/File:Darvasa_gas_crater_panorama.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a>
         </div>Panorama of the site of the <a href="/wiki/Darvaza_gas_crater" title="Darvaza gas crater">Darvaza gas crater</a>
        </div>
       </div>
      </div> 
      <div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">
       Main article: <a href="/wiki/Tourism_in_Turkmenistan" title="Tourism in Turkmenistan">Tourism in Turkmenistan</a>
      </div> 
      <p>Turkmenistan reported arrival of 14,438 foreign tourists in 2019.<sup id="cite_ref-stat2020_99-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-stat2020-99">[99]</a></sup> Turkmenistan's international tourism has not grown significantly despite creation of the <a href="/wiki/Awaza" title="Awaza">Awaza</a> tourist zone on the <a href="/wiki/Caspian_Sea" title="Caspian Sea">Caspian Sea</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-146" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-146">[146]</a></sup> Every traveler must obtain a visa before entering Turkmenistan (see <a href="/wiki/Visa_policy_of_Turkmenistan" title="Visa policy of Turkmenistan">Visa policy of Turkmenistan</a>). To obtain a tourist visa, citizens of most countries need visa support from a local travel agency. For tourists visiting Turkmenistan, organized tours exist providing visits to historical sites in and near <a href="/wiki/Da%C5%9Foguz" title="Daşoguz">Daşoguz</a>, <a href="/wiki/Konye-Urgench" title="Konye-Urgench">Konye-Urgench</a>, <a href="/wiki/Nisa,_Turkmenistan" title="Nisa, Turkmenistan">Nisa</a>, Ancient <a href="/wiki/Merv" title="Merv">Merv</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Mary,_Turkmenistan" title="Mary, Turkmenistan">Mary</a>, as well as beach tours to Avaza and medical tours and holidays in the sanatoria in Mollagara, Bayramaly, Ýylysuw and <a href="/wiki/Archman" title="Archman">Archman</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-sanatoria_147-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-sanatoria-147">[147]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-yylysuw_148-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-yylysuw-148">[148]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-lonely_149-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-lonely-149">[149]</a></sup> </p> 
      <h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Demographics">Demographics</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Turkmenistan&amp;action=edit&amp;section=19" title="Edit section: Demographics">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2> 
      <div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">
       Main article: <a href="/wiki/Demographics_of_Turkmenistan" title="Demographics of Turkmenistan">Demographics of Turkmenistan</a>
      </div>

         <td style="text-align:left;"><a href="/wiki/Ashgabat" title="Ashgabat">Ashgabat</a></td>

         <th colspan="4" style="text-align:center; background:#cfb;">Population<sup id="cite_ref-UN_WPP_150-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-UN_WPP-150">[150]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-UN_WPP_2019_151-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-UN_WPP_2019-151">[151]</a></sup> </th>

      <p>The last census to be published was held in 1995. Results of every census since then have been kept secret. Opposition media reported that some results of the 2012 census had been surreptitiously released, including a total population number of 4,751,120. According to this source, as of 2012 85.6% of the population was ethnically Turkmen, followed by 5.8% ethnic Uzbek and 5.1% ethnic Russian. In contrast, an official Turkmen delegation reported to the <a href="/wiki/UN" class="mw-redirect" title="UN">UN</a> in January 2015 some different figures on national minorities, including slightly under 9% ethnic Uzbek, 2.2% ethnic Russian, and 0.4% ethnic Kazakh. The 2012 census reportedly counted 58 different nationalities.<sup id="cite_ref-chronotm_152-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-chronotm-152">[152]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-jamestown_153-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-jamestown-153">[153]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>Official population estimates of 6.2 million are likely too high, given known emigration trends.<sup id="cite_ref-154" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-154">[154]</a></sup> Population growth has been offset by emigration in search of permanent employment.<sup id="cite_ref-meteo2_155-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-meteo2-155">[155]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>Available figures indicate that most of Turkmenistan's citizens are <a href="/wiki/Turkmen_people" class="mw-redirect" title="Turkmen people">ethnic Turkmens</a> with sizeable minorities of <a href="/wiki/Uzbeks" title="Uzbeks">Uzbeks</a> and <a href="/wiki/Russians" title="Russians">Russians</a>. Smaller minorities include <a href="/wiki/Kazakhs" title="Kazakhs">Kazakhs</a>, <a href="/wiki/Tatars" title="Tatars">Tatars</a>, <a href="/wiki/Ukrainians" title="Ukrainians">Ukrainians</a>, <a href="/wiki/Kurds" title="Kurds">Kurds</a> (native to the <a href="/wiki/Kopet_Dagh" class="mw-redirect" title="Kopet Dagh">Kopet Dagh</a> mountains), <a href="/wiki/Armenians" title="Armenians">Armenians</a>, <a href="/wiki/Azerbaijani_people" class="mw-redirect" title="Azerbaijani people">Azeris</a>, <a href="/wiki/Baloch_people" title="Baloch people">Balochs</a> and <a href="/wiki/Pashtun_people" class="mw-redirect" title="Pashtun people">Pashtuns</a>. The percentage of ethnic <a href="/wiki/Russians_in_Turkmenistan" title="Russians in Turkmenistan">Russians in Turkmenistan</a> dropped from 18.6% in 1939 to 9.5% in 1989. The <i>CIA World Factbook</i> estimated the ethnic composition of Turkmenistan in 2003 as 85% Turkmen, 5% Uzbek, 4% Russian and 6% other.<sup id="cite_ref-World_Factbook_5-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-World_Factbook-5">[5]</a></sup> According to official data announced in Ashgabat in February 2001<sup class="plainlinks noexcerpt noprint asof-tag update" style="display:none;"><a class="external text" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Turkmenistan&amp;action=edit">[update]</a></sup>, 91% of the population were Turkmen, 3% were Uzbeks and 2% were Russians. Between 1989 and 2001 the number of Turkmen in Turkmenistan doubled (from 2.5 to 4.9 million), while the number of Russians dropped by two-thirds (from 334,000 to slightly over 100,000).<sup id="cite_ref-156" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-156">[156]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-census1989_157-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-census1989-157">[157]</a></sup> As of 2021, the number of Russians in Turkmenistan was estimated at approximately 100 thousand.<sup id="cite_ref-meteo_158-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-meteo-158">[158]</a></sup> </p> 
      <h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Migration">Migration</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Turkmenistan&amp;action=edit&amp;section=20" title="Edit section: Migration">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3> 
      <div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">
       See also: <a href="/wiki/Demographics_of_Turkmenistan#Demographic_trends" title="Demographics of Turkmenistan">Demographics of Turkmenistan §&nbsp;Demographic trends</a>
      </div> 
      <p>Based on data from receiving countries, MeteoZhurnal estimated that at least 102,346 Turkmenistani citizens emigrated abroad in 2019, 78% of them to Turkey, and 24,206 apparently returned home, for net migration of 77,014.<sup id="cite_ref-meteo2_155-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-meteo2-155">[155]</a></sup> According to leaked results of a 2018 survey, between 2008 and 2018 1,879,413 Turkmenistani citizens emigrated permanently out of an estimated base population of 5.4 million.<sup id="cite_ref-159" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-159">[159]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-econ_160-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-econ-160">[160]</a></sup> </p> 
      <h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Turkmen_tribes">Turkmen tribes</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Turkmenistan&amp;action=edit&amp;section=21" title="Edit section: Turkmen tribes">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3> 
      <div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">
       Main article: <a href="/wiki/Turkmen_tribes" title="Turkmen tribes">Turkmen tribes</a>
      </div> 
      <p>The tribal nature of Turkmen society is well documented. The major modern Turkmen tribes are <a href="/wiki/Teke_(Turkmen_tribe)" title="Teke (Turkmen tribe)">Teke</a>, <a href="/wiki/Yomut" title="Yomut">Yomut</a>, <a href="/wiki/Ersari" title="Ersari">Ersari</a>, <a href="/wiki/Chowdur" title="Chowdur">Chowdur</a>, Gokleng and Saryk.<sup id="cite_ref-161" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-161">[161]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-162" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-162">[162]</a></sup> The most numerous are the Teke.<sup id="cite_ref-163" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-163">[163]</a></sup> </p> 
      <h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Languages">Languages</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Turkmenistan&amp;action=edit&amp;section=22" title="Edit section: Languages">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2> 
      <div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">
       Main article: <a href="/wiki/Languages_of_Turkmenistan" class="mw-redirect" title="Languages of Turkmenistan">Languages of Turkmenistan</a>
      </div> 
      <p><a href="/wiki/Turkmen_language" title="Turkmen language">Turkmen</a> is the <a href="/wiki/Official_language" title="Official language">official language</a> of Turkmenistan (per the <a href="/wiki/Constitution_of_Turkmenistan" title="Constitution of Turkmenistan">1992 Constitution</a>), although <a href="/wiki/Russian_language" title="Russian language">Russian</a> still is widely spoken in cities as a "language of inter-ethnic communication". Turkmen is spoken by 72% of the population, <a href="/wiki/Russian_language" title="Russian language">Russian</a> by 12% (349,000), <a href="/wiki/Uzbek_language" title="Uzbek language">Uzbek</a> by 9%<sup id="cite_ref-World_Factbook_5-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-World_Factbook-5">[5]</a></sup> (317,000), and other languages by 7% (<a href="/wiki/Kazakh_language" title="Kazakh language">Kazakh</a> (88,000), <a href="/wiki/Tatar_language" title="Tatar language">Tatar</a> (40,400), <a href="/wiki/Ukrainian_language" title="Ukrainian language">Ukrainian</a> (37,118), <a href="/wiki/Azerbaijani_language" title="Azerbaijani language">Azerbaijani</a> (33,000), <a href="/wiki/Armenian_language" title="Armenian language">Armenian</a> (32,000), <a href="/wiki/Northern_Kurdish" class="mw-redirect" title="Northern Kurdish">Northern Kurdish</a> (20,000), <a href="/wiki/Lezgian_language" title="Lezgian language">Lezgian</a> (10,400), <a href="/wiki/Persian_language" title="Persian language">Persian</a> (8,000), <a href="/wiki/Belarusian_language" title="Belarusian language">Belarusian</a> (5,290), <a href="/wiki/Erzya_language" title="Erzya language">Erzya</a> (3,490), <a href="/wiki/Korean_language" title="Korean language">Korean</a> (3,490), <a href="/wiki/Bashkir_language" title="Bashkir language">Bashkir</a> (2,610), <a href="/wiki/Karakalpak_language" title="Karakalpak language">Karakalpak</a> (2,540), <a href="/wiki/Ossetic_language" class="mw-redirect" title="Ossetic language">Ossetic</a> (1,890), <a href="/wiki/Dargwa_language" title="Dargwa language">Dargwa</a> (1,600), <a href="/wiki/Lak_language" title="Lak language">Lak</a> (1,590), <a href="/wiki/Tajik_language" title="Tajik language">Tajik</a> (1,280), <a href="/wiki/Georgian_language" title="Georgian language">Georgian</a> (1,050), <a href="/wiki/Lithuanian_language" title="Lithuanian language">Lithuanian</a> (224), <a href="/wiki/Tabasaran_language" title="Tabasaran language">Tabasaran</a> (180), <a href="/wiki/Dungan_language" title="Dungan language">Dungan</a>).<sup id="cite_ref-164" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-164">[164]</a></sup> </p> 
      <h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Religion">Religion</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Turkmenistan&amp;action=edit&amp;section=23" title="Edit section: Religion">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2> 
      <div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">
       Further information: <a href="/wiki/Religion_in_Turkmenistan" title="Religion in Turkmenistan">Religion in Turkmenistan</a> and <a href="/wiki/Islam_in_Turkmenistan" title="Islam in Turkmenistan">Islam in Turkmenistan</a>
      </div>

      <div class="thumb tright">

         </div><a href="/wiki/Eastern_Orthodoxy_in_Turkmenistan" title="Eastern Orthodoxy in Turkmenistan">Russian Orthodox</a> church in <a href="/wiki/Mary,_Turkmenistan" title="Mary, Turkmenistan">Mary</a>
        </div>
       </div>
      </div> 
      <p>According to the <a href="/wiki/The_World_Factbook" title="The World Factbook">CIA World Factbook</a>, <a href="/wiki/Muslims" title="Muslims">Muslims</a> constitute 93% of the population while 6% of the population are followers of the <a href="/wiki/Eastern_Orthodox_Church" title="Eastern Orthodox Church">Eastern Orthodox Church</a> and the remaining 1% religion is reported as <i>non-religious.</i><sup id="cite_ref-World_Factbook_5-7" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-World_Factbook-5">[5]</a></sup> According to a 2009 <a href="/wiki/Pew_Research_Center" title="Pew Research Center">Pew Research Center</a> report, 93.1% of Turkmenistan's population is Muslim.<sup id="cite_ref-166" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-166">[166]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>The first migrants were sent as <a href="/wiki/Dawah" title="Dawah">missionaries</a> and often were adopted as patriarchs of particular clans or tribal groups, thereby becoming their "founders." Reformulation of communal identity around such figures accounts for one of the highly localized developments of Islamic practice in Turkmenistan.<sup id="cite_ref-JuergensmeyerRoof2011_167-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-JuergensmeyerRoof2011-167">[167]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>In the <a href="/wiki/Soviet_Union" title="Soviet Union">Soviet</a> era, all religious beliefs were attacked by the communist authorities as superstition and "vestiges of the past." Most religious schooling and religious observance were banned, and the vast majority of mosques were closed. However, since 1990, efforts have been made to regain some of the cultural heritage lost under Soviet rule.<sup id="cite_ref-168" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-168">[168]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>Former president <a href="/wiki/Saparmurat_Niyazov" title="Saparmurat Niyazov">Saparmurat Niyazov</a> ordered that basic Islamic principles be taught in public schools. More religious institutions, including religious schools and mosques, have appeared, many with the support of <a href="/wiki/Saudi_Arabia" title="Saudi Arabia">Saudi Arabia</a>, <a href="/wiki/Kuwait" title="Kuwait">Kuwait</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Turkey" title="Turkey">Turkey</a>. Under Niyazov, religious classes were held in both schools and mosques, with instruction in <a href="/wiki/Arabic_language" class="mw-redirect" title="Arabic language">Arabic language</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Qur%27an" class="mw-redirect" title="Qur'an">Qur'an</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Hadith" title="Hadith">hadith</a>, and history of Islam.<sup id="cite_ref-cs2_169-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-cs2-169">[169]</a></sup> At present, the only educational institution teaching religion is the theological faculty of <a href="/wiki/Turkmen_State_University" title="Turkmen State University">Turkmen State University</a>. </p>
      <p>President Niyazov wrote his own religious text, published in separate volumes in 2001 and 2004, entitled the <i><a href="/wiki/Ruhnama" title="Ruhnama">Ruhnama</a></i> ("Book of the Soul"). The Turkmenbashy regime required that the book, which formed the basis of the educational system in Turkmenistan, be given equal status with the Quran (mosques were required to display the two books side by side). The book was heavily promoted as part of the former president's personality cult, and knowledge of the <i>Ruhnama</i> was required even for obtaining a driver's license.<sup id="cite_ref-170" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-170">[170]</a></sup> Quotations from the <i>Ruhnama</i> are inscribed on the walls of the <a href="/wiki/T%C3%BCrkmenba%C5%9Fy_Ruhy_Mosque" title="Türkmenbaşy Ruhy Mosque">Türkmenbaşy Ruhy Mosque</a>, which many Muslims consider sacrilege.<sup id="cite_ref-caravan_171-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-caravan-171">[171]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>Most <a href="/wiki/Christianity_in_Turkmenistan" title="Christianity in Turkmenistan">Christians in Turkmenistan</a> belong to <a href="/wiki/Eastern_Orthodoxy" class="mw-redirect" title="Eastern Orthodoxy">Eastern Orthodoxy</a> (about 5% of the population).<sup id="cite_ref-patriarchia1_172-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-patriarchia1-172">[172]</a></sup> There are 12 Russian Orthodox churches in Turkmenistan, four of which are in <a href="/wiki/Ashgabat" title="Ashgabat">Ashgabat</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-prikhody_173-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-prikhody-173">[173]</a></sup> An archpriest resident in Ashgabat leads the Orthodox Church within the country. Until 2007 Turkmenistan fell under the religious jurisdiction of the Russian Orthodox archbishop in <a href="/wiki/Tashkent" title="Tashkent">Tashkent</a>, <a href="/wiki/Uzbekistan" title="Uzbekistan">Uzbekistan</a>, but since then has been subordinate to the Archbishop of <a href="/wiki/Pyatigorsk" title="Pyatigorsk">Pyatigorsk</a> and <a href="/wiki/Cherkessia" class="mw-redirect" title="Cherkessia">Cherkessia</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-pravo_174-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-pravo-174">[174]</a></sup> There are no Russian Orthodox <a href="/wiki/Seminaries" class="mw-redirect" title="Seminaries">seminaries</a> in Turkmenistan. </p>
      <p>There are also small communities of the following denominations: the <a href="/wiki/Armenian_Apostolic_Church" title="Armenian Apostolic Church">Armenian Apostolic Church</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Roman_Catholic_Church" class="mw-redirect" title="Roman Catholic Church">Roman Catholic Church</a>, <a href="/wiki/Pentecostal_Christians" class="mw-redirect" title="Pentecostal Christians">Pentecostal Christians</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Kale_Heywet_Word_of_Life_Church" class="mw-redirect" title="Kale Heywet Word of Life Church">Protestant Word of Life Church</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Greater_Grace_World_Outreach" title="Greater Grace World Outreach">Greater Grace World Outreach</a> Church, the <a href="/wiki/New_Apostolic_Church" title="New Apostolic Church">New Apostolic Church</a>, <a href="/wiki/Jehovah%27s_Witnesses" title="Jehovah's Witnesses">Jehovah's Witnesses</a>, <a href="/wiki/Judaism" title="Judaism">Jews</a>, and several unaffiliated, nondenominational evangelical Christian groups. In addition, there are small communities of <a href="/wiki/Bah%C3%A1%CA%BC%C3%AD_Faith" title="Baháʼí Faith">Baháʼís</a>, <a href="/wiki/Baptists" title="Baptists">Baptists</a>, <a href="/wiki/Seventh-day_Adventists" class="mw-redirect" title="Seventh-day Adventists">Seventh-day Adventists</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Hare_Krishnas" class="mw-redirect" title="Hare Krishnas">Hare Krishnas</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-state2004_79-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-state2004-79">[79]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>The history of <a href="/wiki/Bah%C3%A1%CA%BC%C3%AD_Faith_in_Turkmenistan" title="Baháʼí Faith in Turkmenistan">Baháʼí Faith in Turkmenistan</a> is as old as the religion itself, and Baháʼí communities still exist today.<sup id="cite_ref-175" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-175">[175]</a></sup> The first <a href="/wiki/Bah%C3%A1%CA%BC%C3%AD_House_of_Worship" title="Baháʼí House of Worship">Baháʼí House of Worship</a> was built in Ashgabat at the beginning of the twentieth century. It was seized by the Soviets in the 1920s and converted to an art gallery. It was heavily damaged in the earthquake of 1948 and later demolished. The site was converted to a public park.<sup id="cite_ref-176" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-176">[176]</a></sup> </p> 
      <h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Syncretism_of_Islam_and_pre-Islamic_Turkic_beliefs">Syncretism of Islam and pre-Islamic Turkic beliefs</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Turkmenistan&amp;action=edit&amp;section=24" title="Edit section: Syncretism of Islam and pre-Islamic Turkic beliefs">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3> 
      <div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">
       Main article: <a href="/wiki/Religion_in_Turkmenistan#Syncretism_in_Turkmen_Islam" title="Religion in Turkmenistan">Religion in Turkmenistan §&nbsp;Syncretism in Turkmen Islam</a>
      </div> 
      <p>The Russian Academy of Sciences has identified many instances of <a href="/wiki/Syncretism#Religious_syncretism" title="Syncretism">syncretic</a> influence of pre-Islamic <a href="/wiki/Turkic_peoples" title="Turkic peoples">Turkic</a> belief systems on practice of Islam among Turkmen.<sup id="cite_ref-ras_177-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ras-177">[177]</a></sup> </p> 
      <h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Culture">Culture</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Turkmenistan&amp;action=edit&amp;section=25" title="Edit section: Culture">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2> 
      <div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">
       Main article: <a href="/wiki/Culture_of_Turkmenistan" title="Culture of Turkmenistan">Culture of Turkmenistan</a>
      </div> 
      <h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Heritage">Heritage</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Turkmenistan&amp;action=edit&amp;section=26" title="Edit section: Heritage">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
